<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale_(disambiguation)" title="Florence Nightingale (disambiguation)">Florence Nightingale (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<div class="description">Recorded to wax cylinder on 30 July 1890, to raise money for veterans of the <a href="/wiki/Charge_of_the_Light_Brigade" title="Charge of the Light Brigade">Charge of the Light Brigade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
</div></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Florence Nightingale</b> <span class="noexcerpt nowraplinks" style="font-size:85%; font-weight:normal;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Order_of_Merit" title="Member of the Order of Merit">OM</a> <a href="/wiki/Royal_Red_Cross" title="Royal Red Cross">RRC</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dame_of_Grace_of_the_Order_of_St_John" title="Dame of Grace of the Order of St John">DStJ</a></span> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ŋ/: 'ng' in 'sing'">ŋ</span><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span></span>/</a></span></span>; 12 May 1820 – 13 August 1910) was an English <a href="/wiki/Reform_movement" title="Reform movement">social reformer</a>, statistician and the founder of modern <a href="/wiki/Nursing" title="Nursing">nursing</a>. Nightingale came to prominence while serving as a manager and trainer of nurses during the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a>, in which she organised care for wounded soldiers at <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> She significantly reduced death rates by improving hygiene and living standards. Nightingale gave nursing a favourable reputation and became an icon of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victorian_culture" title="Victorian culture">Victorian culture</a>, especially in the persona of "The Lady with the Lamp" making rounds of wounded soldiers at night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parragon_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parragon-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent commentators have asserted that Nightingale's Crimean War achievements were exaggerated by the media at the time, but critics agree on the importance of her later work in professionalising nursing roles for women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_2017_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_2017-7">[7]</a></sup> In 1860, she laid the foundation of professional nursing with the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale_Faculty_of_Nursing_and_Midwifery" title="Florence Nightingale Faculty of Nursing and Midwifery">her nursing school</a> at <a href="/wiki/St_Thomas%27_Hospital" title="St Thomas' Hospital">St Thomas' Hospital</a> in London. It was the first secular nursing school in the world and is now part of <a href="/wiki/King%27s_College_London" title="King's College London">King's College London</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> In recognition of her pioneering work in nursing, the <a href="/wiki/Nightingale_Pledge" title="Nightingale Pledge">Nightingale Pledge</a> taken by new nurses, and the <a href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale_Medal" title="Florence Nightingale Medal">Florence Nightingale Medal</a>, the highest international distinction a nurse can achieve, were named in her honour, and the annual <a href="/wiki/International_Nurses_Day" title="International Nurses Day">International Nurses Day</a> is celebrated on her birthday. Her social reforms included improving healthcare for all sections of British society, advocating better hunger relief in India, helping to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abolitionism_(prostitution)" title="Abolitionism (prostitution)">abolish prostitution laws</a> that were harsh for women, and expanding the acceptable forms of female participation in the workforce.
</p><p>Nightingale was a pioneer in <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a>; she represented her analysis in graphical forms to ease drawing conclusions and actionables from data. She is famous for usage of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_area_diagram" title="Polar area diagram">polar area diagram</a>, also called the Nightingale rose diagram, equivalent to a modern circular <a href="/wiki/Histogram" title="Histogram">histogram</a>. This diagram is still regularly used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Data_visualization" title="Data visualization">data visualisation</a>.
</p><p>Nightingale was a prodigious and versatile writer. In her lifetime, much of her published work was concerned with spreading medical knowledge. Some of her tracts were written in <a href="/wiki/Plain_English" title="Plain English">simple English</a> so that they could easily be understood by those with poor literary skills. She was also a pioneer in data visualization with the use of <a href="/wiki/Infographic" title="Infographic">infographics</a>, using graphical presentations of statistical data in an effective way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_2017_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_2017-7">[7]</a></sup> Much of her writing, including her extensive work on religion and <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">mysticism</a>, has only been published posthumously.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Embley_Park.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="576" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Embley_Park.jpg/220px-Embley_Park.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Embley_Park.jpg/330px-Embley_Park.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Embley_Park.jpg/440px-Embley_Park.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Embley_Park.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Embley_Park" title="Embley Park">Embley Park</a> in Hampshire, now a school, one of the family homes of <a href="/wiki/William_Nightingale" title="William Nightingale">William Nightingale</a></div></div></div>
<p>Florence Nightingale was born on 12 May 1820 into a wealthy and well-connected British family at the <i>Villa Colombaia</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>, Tuscany, Italy, and was named after the city of her birth. Florence's older sister <a href="/wiki/Frances_Parthenope_Verney" title="Frances Parthenope Verney">Frances Parthenope</a> had similarly been named after her place of birth, <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Naples" title="History of Naples">Parthenope</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> settlement now part of the city of <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a>. The family moved back to England in 1821, with Nightingale being brought up in the family's homes at <a href="/wiki/Embley,_Hampshire" title="Embley, Hampshire">Embley, Hampshire</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dethick,_Lea_and_Holloway" title="Dethick, Lea and Holloway">Lea Hurst, Derbyshire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NightingaleonMysticism_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-NightingaleonMysticism-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NightingaleCrimeanColectedWorkds_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-NightingaleCrimeanColectedWorkds-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Florence inherited a liberal-humanitarian outlook from both sides of her family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_2017_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_2017-7">[7]</a></sup> Her parents were <a href="/wiki/William_Nightingale" title="William Nightingale">William Edward Nightingale, born William Edward Shore</a> (1794–1874) and Frances ("Fanny") Nightingale (<span title="Name at birth"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N%C3%A9e" title="Née">née</a></span> Smith; 1788–1880). William's mother Mary (<span title="Name at birth"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N%C3%A9e" title="Née">née</a></span> Evans) was the niece of Peter Nightingale, under the terms of whose will William inherited his estate at Lea Hurst, and assumed the name and arms of Nightingale. Fanny's father (Florence's maternal grandfather) was the <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Abolitionism in the United Kingdom">abolitionist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarian</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Smith_(abolitionist)" title="William Smith (abolitionist)">William Smith</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Nightingale's father educated her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NightingaleCrimeanColectedWorkds_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-NightingaleCrimeanColectedWorkds-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> documentary reported that "Florence and her older sister Parthenope benefited from their father's advanced ideas about women's education. They studied history, mathematics, Italian, classical literature, and philosophy, and from an early age Florence, who was the more academic of the two girls, displayed an extraordinary ability for collecting and analysing data which she would use to great effect in later life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_2017_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_2017-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Florence_Nightingale_-_Project_Gutenberg_13103.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="391" data-file-width="302" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Florence_Nightingale_-_Project_Gutenberg_13103.jpg/170px-Florence_Nightingale_-_Project_Gutenberg_13103.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Florence_Nightingale_-_Project_Gutenberg_13103.jpg/255px-Florence_Nightingale_-_Project_Gutenberg_13103.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5b/Florence_Nightingale_-_Project_Gutenberg_13103.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Florence_Nightingale_-_Project_Gutenberg_13103.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Young Florence Nightingale</div></div></div>
<p>In 1838, her father took the family on a tour in Europe where she was introduced to the English-born Parisian hostess <a href="/wiki/Mary_Elizabeth_Mohl" title="Mary Elizabeth Mohl">Mary Clarke</a>, with whom Florence bonded. She recorded that "Clarkey" was a stimulating hostess who did not care for her appearance, and while her ideas did not always agree with those of her guests, "she was incapable of boring anyone." Her behaviour was said to be exasperating and eccentric and she had little respect for upper-class British women, whom she regarded generally as inconsequential. She said that if given the choice between being a woman or a galley slave, then she would choose the freedom of the galleys. She generally rejected female company and spent her time with male intellectuals. Clarke made an exception, however, in the case of the Nightingale family and Florence in particular. She and Florence were to remain close friends for 40 years despite their 27-year age difference. Clarke demonstrated that women could be equals to men, an idea that Florence had not obtained from her mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cromwell_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-cromwell-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nightingale underwent the first of several experiences that she believed were calls from God in February 1837 while at <a href="/wiki/Embley_Park" title="Embley Park">Embley Park</a>, prompting a strong desire to devote her life to the service of others. In her youth she was respectful of her family's opposition to her working as a nurse, only announcing her decision to enter the field in 1844. Despite the anger and distress of her mother and sister, she rejected the expected role for a woman of her status to become a wife and mother. Nightingale worked hard to educate herself in the art and science of nursing, in the face of opposition from her family and the restrictive social code for affluent young English women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Small's_bio_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Small's_bio-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Florence_Nightingale_by_Augustus_Egg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2484" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Florence_Nightingale_by_Augustus_Egg.jpg/170px-Florence_Nightingale_by_Augustus_Egg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Florence_Nightingale_by_Augustus_Egg.jpg/255px-Florence_Nightingale_by_Augustus_Egg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Florence_Nightingale_by_Augustus_Egg.jpg/340px-Florence_Nightingale_by_Augustus_Egg.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Florence_Nightingale_by_Augustus_Egg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Painting of Nightingale by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augustus_Egg" title="Augustus Egg">Augustus Egg</a>, c. 1840s</div></div></div>
<p>As a young woman, Nightingale was described as attractive, slender, and graceful. While her demeanour was often severe, she was said to be very charming and to possess a radiant smile. Her most persistent suitor was the politician and poet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richard_Monckton_Milnes" title="Richard Monckton Milnes">Richard Monckton Milnes</a>, but after a nine-year courtship, she rejected him, convinced that marriage would interfere with her ability to follow her calling to nursing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Small's_bio_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Small's_bio-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Rome in 1847, she met <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Herbert,_1st_Baron_Herbert_of_Lea" title="Sidney Herbert, 1st Baron Herbert of Lea">Sidney Herbert</a>, a politician who had been <a href="/wiki/Secretary_at_War" title="Secretary at War">Secretary at War</a> (1845–1846) who was on his honeymoon. He and Nightingale became lifelong close friends. Herbert would be Secretary of War again during the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> when he and his wife would be instrumental in facilitating Nightingale's nursing work in Crimea. She became Herbert's key adviser throughout his political career, though she was accused by some of having hastened Herbert's death from <a href="/wiki/Bright%27s_disease" title="Bright's disease">Bright's disease</a> in 1861 because of the pressure her programme of reform placed on him. Nightingale also much later had strong relations with academic <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Jowett" title="Benjamin Jowett">Benjamin Jowett</a>, who may have wanted to marry her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Florence_Nightingale_by_Kilburn_c1854.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="482" data-file-width="311" decoding="async" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Florence_Nightingale_by_Kilburn_c1854.jpg/170px-Florence_Nightingale_by_Kilburn_c1854.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Florence_Nightingale_by_Kilburn_c1854.jpg/255px-Florence_Nightingale_by_Kilburn_c1854.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0f/Florence_Nightingale_by_Kilburn_c1854.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Florence_Nightingale_by_Kilburn_c1854.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nightingale c. 1854</div></div></div>
<p>Nightingale continued her travels (now with Charles and <a href="/wiki/Selina_Bracebridge" title="Selina Bracebridge">Selina Bracebridge</a>) as far as Greece and Egypt. While in Athens, Greece, Nightingale rescued a juvenile <a href="/wiki/Little_owl" title="Little owl">little owl</a> from a group of children who were tormenting it, and she named the owl Athena. Nightingale often carried the owl in her pocket, until the pet died (soon before Nightingale left for Crimea).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Her writings on Egypt, in particular, are testimony to her learning, literary skill, and philosophy of life. Sailing up the Nile as far as Abu Simbel in January 1850, she wrote of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Simbel_temples" title="Abu Simbel temples">Abu Simbel temples</a>, "Sublime in the highest style of intellectual beauty, intellect without effort, without suffering ... not a feature is correct — but the whole effect is more expressive of spiritual grandeur than anything I could have imagined. It makes the impression upon one that thousands of voices do, uniting in one unanimous simultaneous feeling of enthusiasm or emotion, which is said to overcome the strongest man."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaney39-74_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chaney39-74-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>At Thebes, she wrote of being "called to God", while a week later near Cairo she wrote in her diary (as distinct from her far longer letters that her elder sister Parthenope was to print after her return): "God called me in the morning and asked me would I do good for him alone without reputation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaney39-74_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chaney39-74-18">[18]</a></sup> Later in 1850, she visited the Lutheran religious community at <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf-Kaiserswerth" title="Düsseldorf-Kaiserswerth">Kaiserswerth-am-Rhein</a> in Germany, where she observed Pastor <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Fliedner" title="Theodor Fliedner">Theodor Fliedner</a> and the deaconesses working for the sick and the deprived. She regarded the experience as a turning point in her life, and issued her findings anonymously in 1851; <i>The Institution of Kaiserswerth on the Rhine, for the Practical Training of Deaconesses, etc.</i> was her first published work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> She also received four months of medical training at the institute, which formed the basis for her later care.
</p><p>On 22 August 1853, Nightingale took the post of superintendent at the <a href="/wiki/Nightingale_Hospital_(Marylebone)" title="Nightingale Hospital (Marylebone)">Institute for the Care of Sick Gentlewomen</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_Harley_Street" title="Upper Harley Street">Upper Harley Street</a>, London, a position she held until October 1854.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hsguide_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-hsguide-20">[20]</a></sup> Her father had given her an annual income of £500 (roughly £40,000/US$65,000 in present terms), which allowed her to live comfortably and to pursue her career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Crimean_War">Crimean War</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Notes_on_Nursing_(28).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="422" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Notes_on_Nursing_%2828%29.jpg/220px-Notes_on_Nursing_%2828%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Notes_on_Nursing_%2828%29.jpg/330px-Notes_on_Nursing_%2828%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Notes_on_Nursing_%2828%29.jpg/440px-Notes_on_Nursing_%2828%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Notes_on_Nursing_(28).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A print of the jewel awarded to Nightingale by <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a>, for her services to the soldiers in the war</div></div></div>
<p>Florence Nightingale's most famous contribution came during the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a>, which became her central focus when reports got back to Britain about the horrific conditions for the wounded at the military hospital on the Asiatic side of the <a href="/wiki/Bosporus" title="Bosporus">Bosporus</a>, opposite <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, at Scutari (modern-day <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Csk%C3%BCdar" title="Üsküdar">Üsküdar</a> in <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>). Britain and France entered the war against Russia on the side of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. On 21 October 1854, she and the staff of 38 women volunteer nurses including her head nurse <a href="/wiki/Eliza_Roberts_(British_nurse)" title="Eliza Roberts (British nurse)">Eliza Roberts</a> and her aunt Mai Smith,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> and 15 Catholic nuns (mobilised by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Edward_Manning" title="Henry Edward Manning">Henry Edward Manning</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> were sent (under the authorisation of Sidney Herbert) to the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. On the way, Nightingale was assisted in Paris by her friend <a href="/wiki/Mary_Elizabeth_Mohl" title="Mary Elizabeth Mohl">Mary Clarke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The volunteer nurses worked about 295 <a href="/wiki/Nautical_mile" title="Nautical mile">nautical miles</a> (546 km; 339 mi) away from the main British camp across the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> at <a href="/wiki/Balaklava" title="Balaklava">Balaklava</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:151px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nightingale_letter.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="242" data-file-width="149" decoding="async" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/df/Nightingale_letter.jpeg" width="149"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nightingale_letter.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Letter from Nightingale to <a href="/wiki/Mary_Elizabeth_Mohl" title="Mary Elizabeth Mohl">Mary Mohl</a>, 1881</div></div></div>
<p>Nightingale arrived at <a href="/wiki/Selimiye_Barracks" title="Selimiye Barracks">Selimiye Barracks</a> in Scutari early in November 1854. Her team found that poor care for wounded soldiers was being delivered by overworked medical staff in the face of official indifference. Medicines were in short supply, <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygiene</a> was being neglected, and mass infections were common, many of them fatal. There was no equipment to process food for the patients.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>This frail young woman ... embraced in her solicitude the sick of three armies.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="/wiki/Lucien_Baudens" title="Lucien Baudens">Lucien Baudens</a>, <i>La guerre de Crimée, les campements, les abris, les ambulances, les hôpitaux </i>, p. 104.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>After Nightingale sent a plea to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> for a government solution to the poor condition of the facilities, the British Government commissioned <a href="/wiki/Isambard_Kingdom_Brunel" title="Isambard Kingdom Brunel">Isambard Kingdom Brunel</a> to design a <a href="/wiki/Prefabricated_building" title="Prefabricated building">prefabricated</a> hospital that could be built in England and shipped to the <a href="/wiki/Dardanelles" title="Dardanelles">Dardanelles</a>. The result was <a href="/wiki/Renkioi_Hospital" title="Renkioi Hospital">Renkioi Hospital</a>, a civilian facility that, under the management of <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Alexander_Parkes" title="Edmund Alexander Parkes">Edmund Alexander Parkes</a>, had a death rate less than one tenth of that of Scutari.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Stephen_Paget" title="Stephen Paget">Stephen Paget</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/Dictionary_of_National_Biography" title="Dictionary of National Biography">Dictionary of National Biography</a></i> asserted that Nightingale reduced the death rate from 42% to 2%, either by making improvements in hygiene herself, or by calling for the Sanitary Commission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> For example, Nightingale implemented <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Handwashing" title="Handwashing">handwashing</a> and other hygiene practices in the war hospital in which she worked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coloured_mezzotint;_Florence_Nightingale,_Wellcome_L0019661.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4634" data-file-width="3687" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Coloured_mezzotint%3B_Florence_Nightingale%2C_Wellcome_L0019661.jpg/170px-Coloured_mezzotint%3B_Florence_Nightingale%2C_Wellcome_L0019661.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Coloured_mezzotint%3B_Florence_Nightingale%2C_Wellcome_L0019661.jpg/255px-Coloured_mezzotint%3B_Florence_Nightingale%2C_Wellcome_L0019661.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Coloured_mezzotint%3B_Florence_Nightingale%2C_Wellcome_L0019661.jpg/340px-Coloured_mezzotint%3B_Florence_Nightingale%2C_Wellcome_L0019661.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coloured_mezzotint;_Florence_Nightingale,_Wellcome_L0019661.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Florence Nightingale, <i>an angel of mercy</i>. <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Csk%C3%BCdar" title="Üsküdar">Scutari</a> hospital 1855.</div></div></div>
<p>During her first winter at Scutari, 4,077 soldiers died there. Ten times more soldiers died from illnesses such as <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Typhoid" title="Typhoid">typhoid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dysentery" title="Dysentery">dysentery</a> than from battle wounds. With overcrowding, defective <a href="/wiki/Sanitary_sewer" title="Sanitary sewer">sewers</a> and lack of ventilation, the Sanitary Commission had to be sent out by the British government to Scutari in March 1855, almost six months after Nightingale had arrived. The commission flushed out the sewers and improved ventilation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Death rates were sharply reduced, but she never claimed credit for helping to reduce the death rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Constable1998_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Constable1998-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Florence_Nightingale_to_Her_Nurses_(1914)_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Florence_Nightingale_to_Her_Nurses_(1914)-31">[31]</a></sup> Head Nurse <a href="/wiki/Eliza_Roberts_(British_nurse)" title="Eliza Roberts (British nurse)">Eliza Roberts</a> nursed Nightingale through her critical illness of May 1855.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2001 and 2008 the BBC released documentaries that were critical of Nightingale's performance in the Crimean War, as were some follow-up articles published in <i>The Guardian</i> and the <i>Sunday Times</i>. Nightingale scholar <a href="/wiki/Lynn_McDonald" title="Lynn McDonald">Lynn McDonald</a> has dismissed these criticisms as "often preposterous", arguing they are not supported by the primary sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NightingaleCrimeanColectedWorkds_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-NightingaleCrimeanColectedWorkds-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nightingale still believed that the death rates were due to poor nutrition, lack of supplies, stale air, and overworking of the soldiers. After she returned to Britain and began collecting evidence before the Royal Commission on the Health of the Army, she came to believe that most of the soldiers at the hospital were killed by poor living conditions. This experience influenced her later career when she advocated sanitary living conditions as of great importance. Consequently, she reduced peacetime deaths in the army and turned her attention to the sanitary design of hospitals and the introduction of sanitation in working-class homes (see Statistics and Sanitary Reform, below).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Small_2017_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Small_2017-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nightingale_receiving_the_Wounded_at_Scutari_by_Jerry_BarrettFXD.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Nightingale_receiving_the_Wounded_at_Scutari_by_Jerry_BarrettFXD.jpg/220px-Nightingale_receiving_the_Wounded_at_Scutari_by_Jerry_BarrettFXD.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Nightingale_receiving_the_Wounded_at_Scutari_by_Jerry_BarrettFXD.jpg/330px-Nightingale_receiving_the_Wounded_at_Scutari_by_Jerry_BarrettFXD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Nightingale_receiving_the_Wounded_at_Scutari_by_Jerry_BarrettFXD.jpg/440px-Nightingale_receiving_the_Wounded_at_Scutari_by_Jerry_BarrettFXD.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nightingale_receiving_the_Wounded_at_Scutari_by_Jerry_BarrettFXD.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Mission of Mercy: Florence Nightingale receiving the Wounded at Scutari</i> (<a href="/wiki/Jerry_Barrett" title="Jerry Barrett">Jerry Barrett</a>, 1857)</div></div></div>
<p>According to some secondary sources, Nightingale had a frosty relationship with her fellow nurse <a href="/wiki/Mary_Seacole" title="Mary Seacole">Mary Seacole</a>, who ran a hotel/hospital for officers. Seacole's own memoir, <i>Wonderful Adventures of Mrs. Seacole in Many Lands</i>, records only one, friendly, meeting with her, when she asked her for a bed for the night, and got it; Seacole was in Scutari en route to the Crimea to join her business partner and start their business. However, Seacole pointed out that when she tried to join Nightingale's group, one of Nightingale's colleagues rebuffed her, and Seacole inferred that racism was at the root of that rebuttal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Nightingale told her brother-in-law, in a private letter, that she was worried about contact between her work and Seacole's business, claiming that while “she was very kind to the men and, what is more, to the Officers – and did some good (she) made many drunk”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Nightingale reportedly wrote, "I had the greatest difficulty in repelling Mrs. Seacole's advances, and in preventing association between her and my nurses (absolutely out of the question!)...Anyone who employs Mrs. Seacole will introduce much kindness – also much drunkenness and improper conduct".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> On the other hand, Seacole told the French chef <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Soyer" title="Alexis Soyer">Alexis Soyer</a> that "You must know, M Soyer, that Miss Nightingale is very fond of me. When I passed through Scutari, she very kindly gave me board and lodging."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>The arrival of two waves of Irish nuns, the <a href="/wiki/Sisters_of_Mercy" title="Sisters of Mercy">Sisters of Mercy</a> to assist with nursing duties at Scutari met with different responses from Nightingale. <a href="/wiki/Mary_Clare_Moore" title="Mary Clare Moore">Mary Clare Moore</a> headed the first wave and placed herself and her Sisters under the authority of Nightingale. The two were to remain friends for the rest of their lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The second wave, headed by <a href="/wiki/Mary_Francis_Bridgeman" title="Mary Francis Bridgeman">Mary Francis Bridgeman</a> met with a cooler reception as Bridgeman refused to give up her authority over her Sisters to Nightingale while at the same time not trusting Nightingale, whom she regarded as ambitious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beyond_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beyond-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Lady_with_the_Lamp">The Lady with the Lamp</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Florence_Nightingale._Coloured_lithograph._Wellcome_V0006579.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3008" data-file-width="2362" decoding="async" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Florence_Nightingale._Coloured_lithograph._Wellcome_V0006579.jpg/220px-Florence_Nightingale._Coloured_lithograph._Wellcome_V0006579.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Florence_Nightingale._Coloured_lithograph._Wellcome_V0006579.jpg/330px-Florence_Nightingale._Coloured_lithograph._Wellcome_V0006579.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Florence_Nightingale._Coloured_lithograph._Wellcome_V0006579.jpg/440px-Florence_Nightingale._Coloured_lithograph._Wellcome_V0006579.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Florence_Nightingale._Coloured_lithograph._Wellcome_V0006579.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miss_Nightingale_at_Scutari_(1854)" title="Miss Nightingale at Scutari (1854)">The Lady with the Lamp</a></i>. Popular lithograph reproduction of a painting of Nightingale by <a href="/wiki/Henrietta_Rae" title="Henrietta Rae">Henrietta Rae</a>, 1891.</div></div></div>
<p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crimean_war" title="Crimean war">Crimean war</a>, Nightingale gained the nickname "The Lady with the Lamp" from a phrase in a report in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i>:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>She is a "ministering angel" without any exaggeration in these hospitals, and as her slender form glides quietly along each corridor, every poor fellow's face softens with gratitude at the sight of her. When all the medical officers have retired for the night and silence and darkness have settled down upon those miles of prostrate sick, she may be observed alone, with a little lamp in her hand, making her solitary rounds.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Cited in Cook, E. T. <i>The Life of Florence Nightingale</i>. (1913) Vol 1, p 237.</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>The phrase was further popularised by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wadsworth_Longfellow" title="Henry Wadsworth Longfellow">Henry Wadsworth Longfellow</a>'s 1857 poem "Santa Filomena":<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><div class="poem">
<p>Lo! in that house of misery<br/>
A lady with a lamp I see<br/>
Pass through the glimmering gloom,<br/>
And flit from room to room.
</p>
</div></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_career">Later career</span></h2>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a> on 29 November 1855, the Nightingale Fund was established for the training of nurses during a public meeting to recognise Nightingale for her work in the war. There was an outpouring of generous donations. Sidney Herbert served as honorary secretary of the fund and the <a href="/wiki/Prince_George,_Duke_of_Cambridge" title="Prince George, Duke of Cambridge">Duke of Cambridge</a> was chairman. In her 1856 letters she described spas in the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, detailing the health conditions, physical descriptions, dietary information, and other vital details of patients whom she directed there. She noted that the treatment there was significantly less expensive than in Switzerland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Florence_Nightingale_by_Goodman,_1858.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1982" data-file-width="1324" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Florence_Nightingale_by_Goodman%2C_1858.jpg/170px-Florence_Nightingale_by_Goodman%2C_1858.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Florence_Nightingale_by_Goodman%2C_1858.jpg/255px-Florence_Nightingale_by_Goodman%2C_1858.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Florence_Nightingale_by_Goodman%2C_1858.jpg/340px-Florence_Nightingale_by_Goodman%2C_1858.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Florence_Nightingale_by_Goodman,_1858.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nightingale, c. 1858, by Goodman</div></div></div>
<p>Nightingale had £45,000 at her disposal from the Nightingale Fund to set up the first nursing school, the Nightingale Training School, at <a href="/wiki/St_Thomas%27_Hospital" title="St Thomas' Hospital">St Thomas' Hospital</a> on 9 July 1860.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The first trained Nightingale nurses began work on 16 May 1865 at the Liverpool Workhouse Infirmary. Now called the <a href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale_Faculty_of_Nursing_and_Midwifery" title="Florence Nightingale Faculty of Nursing and Midwifery">Florence Nightingale Faculty of Nursing and Midwifery</a>, the school is part of <a href="/wiki/King%27s_College_London" title="King's College London">King's College London</a>. In 1866 she said the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Buckinghamshire_Hospital" title="Royal Buckinghamshire Hospital">Royal Buckinghamshire Hospital</a> in <a href="/wiki/Aylesbury" title="Aylesbury">Aylesbury</a> near her sister's home <a href="/wiki/Claydon_House" title="Claydon House">Claydon House</a> would be "the most beautiful hospital in England", and in 1868 called it "an excellent model to follow".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nightingale wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Notes_on_Nursing" title="Notes on Nursing">Notes on Nursing</a></i> (1859). The book served as the cornerstone of the curriculum at the Nightingale School and other nursing schools, though it was written specifically for the education of those nursing at home. Nightingale wrote, "Every day sanitary knowledge, or the knowledge of nursing, or in other words, of how to put the constitution in such a state as that it will have no disease, or that it can recover from disease, takes a higher place. It is recognised as the knowledge which every one ought to have – distinct from medical knowledge, which only a profession can have".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FlorenceNightingale_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FlorenceNightingale-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Notes on Nursing</i> also sold well to the general reading public and is considered a classic introduction to nursing. Nightingale spent the rest of her life promoting and organising the nursing profession. In the introduction to the 1974 edition, Joan Quixley of the Nightingale School of Nursing wrote: "The book was the first of its kind ever to be written. It appeared at a time when the simple rules of health were only beginning to be known, when its topics were of vital importance not only for the well-being and recovery of patients, when hospitals were riddled with infection, when nurses were still mainly regarded as ignorant, uneducated persons. The book has, inevitably, its place in the history of nursing, for it was written by the founder of modern nursing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-QuixleyonNightingale_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-QuixleyonNightingale-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Chuzzlewit_illus11.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2564" data-file-width="1816" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Martin_Chuzzlewit_illus11.jpg/170px-Martin_Chuzzlewit_illus11.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Martin_Chuzzlewit_illus11.jpg/255px-Martin_Chuzzlewit_illus11.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Martin_Chuzzlewit_illus11.jpg/340px-Martin_Chuzzlewit_illus11.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Chuzzlewit_illus11.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration in Charles Dickens' <i><a href="/wiki/Martin_Chuzzlewit" title="Martin Chuzzlewit">Martin Chuzzlewit</a></i>. Nurse Sarah Gamp (left) became a stereotype of untrained and incompetent nurses of the early Victorian era, before the reforms of Nightingale</div></div></div>
<p>As <a href="/wiki/Mark_Bostridge" title="Mark Bostridge">Mark Bostridge</a> has demonstrated, one of Nightingale's signal achievements was the introduction of trained nurses into the <a href="/wiki/Workhouse" title="Workhouse">workhouse</a> system in Britain from the 1860s onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bostridge_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bostridge-47">[47]</a></sup> This meant that sick paupers were no longer being cared for by other, able-bodied paupers, but by properly trained nursing staff. In the first half of the 19th century, nurses were usually former servants or widows who found no other job and therefore were forced to earn their living by this work. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a> caricatured the standard of care in his 1842–1843 published novel <i><a href="/wiki/Martin_Chuzzlewit" title="Martin Chuzzlewit">Martin Chuzzlewit</a></i> in the figure of <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Gamp" title="Sarah Gamp">Sarah Gamp</a> as being incompetent, negligent, alcoholic and corrupt. According to Caroline Worthington, director of the <a href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale_Museum" title="Florence Nightingale Museum">Florence Nightingale Museum</a>, "When she [Nightingale] started out there was no such thing as nursing. The Dickens character Sarah Gamp, who was more interested in drinking gin than looking after her patients, was only a mild exaggeration. Hospitals were places of last resort where the floors were laid with straw to soak up the blood. Florence transformed nursing when she got back [from Crimea]. She had access to people in high places and she used it to get things done. Florence was stubborn, opinionated, and forthright but she had to be those things in order to achieve all that she did."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Express_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Express-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Though Nightingale is sometimes said to have denied the theory of infection for her entire life, a 2008 biography disagrees,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bostridge_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bostridge-47">[47]</a></sup> saying that she was simply opposed to a precursor of germ theory known as <a href="/wiki/Contingent_contagionism" title="Contingent contagionism">contagionism</a>. This theory held that diseases could only be transmitted by touch. Before the experiments of the mid-1860s by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Pasteur</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Lister,_1st_Baron_Lister" title="Joseph Lister, 1st Baron Lister">Lister</a>, hardly anyone took germ theory seriously; even afterwards, many medical practitioners were unconvinced. Bostridge points out that in the early 1880s Nightingale wrote an article for a textbook in which she advocated strict precautions designed, she said, to kill germs. Nightingale's work served as an inspiration for nurses in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Union_(American_Civil_War)" title="Union (American Civil War)">Union</a> government approached her for advice in organising field medicine. Her ideas inspired the volunteer body of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Sanitary_Commission" title="United States Sanitary Commission">United States Sanitary Commission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Florence_nightingale_at_st_thomas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="346" data-file-width="516" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Florence_nightingale_at_st_thomas.jpg/220px-Florence_nightingale_at_st_thomas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Florence_nightingale_at_st_thomas.jpg/330px-Florence_nightingale_at_st_thomas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Florence_nightingale_at_st_thomas.jpg/440px-Florence_nightingale_at_st_thomas.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Florence_nightingale_at_st_thomas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Florence Nightingale (middle) in 1886 with her graduating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Florence_nightingale_school_of_nursing_and_midwifery" title="Florence nightingale school of nursing and midwifery">class of nurses</a> from <a href="/wiki/St_Thomas%27_Hospital" title="St Thomas' Hospital">St Thomas'</a> outside <a href="/wiki/Claydon_House" title="Claydon House">Claydon House</a>, Buckinghamshire</div></div></div>
<p>In the 1870s, Nightingale mentored <a href="/wiki/Linda_Richards" title="Linda Richards">Linda Richards</a>, "America's first trained nurse", and enabled her to return to the United States with adequate training and knowledge to establish high-quality nursing schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Richards went on to become a nursing pioneer in the US and Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1882, several Nightingale nurses had become matrons at several leading hospitals, including, in London (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St_Mary%27s_Hospital_(London)" title="St Mary's Hospital (London)">St Mary's Hospital</a>, Westminster Hospital, St Marylebone Workhouse Infirmary and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Hospital_for_Neuro-disability" title="Royal Hospital for Neuro-disability">Hospital for Incurables</a> at <a href="/wiki/Putney" title="Putney">Putney</a>) and throughout Britain (<a href="/wiki/Netley_Hospital" title="Netley Hospital">Royal Victoria Hospital</a>, <a href="/wiki/Netley" title="Netley">Netley</a>; <a href="/wiki/Royal_Infirmary_of_Edinburgh" title="Royal Infirmary of Edinburgh">Edinburgh Royal Infirmary</a>; Cumberland Infirmary and Liverpool Royal Infirmary), as well as at <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Hospital" title="Sydney Hospital">Sydney Hospital</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">New South Wales</a>, Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1883, Nightingale became the first recipient of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Red_Cross" title="Royal Red Cross">Royal Red Cross</a>. In 1904, she was appointed a <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Saint_John_(chartered_1888)" title="Order of Saint John (chartered 1888)">Lady of Grace of the Order of St John (LGStJ)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1907, she became the first woman to be awarded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Order_of_Merit_(Commonwealth)" title="Order of Merit (Commonwealth)">Order of Merit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> In the following year she was given the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_City" title="Freedom of the City">Honorary Freedom</a> of the <a href="/wiki/City_of_London" title="City of London">City of London</a>. Her birthday is now celebrated as International <a href="/wiki/Chronic_fatigue_syndrome" title="Chronic fatigue syndrome">CFS</a> Awareness Day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>From 1857 onwards, Nightingale was intermittently bedridden and suffered from depression. A recent biography cites <a href="/wiki/Brucellosis" title="Brucellosis">brucellosis</a> and associated <a href="/wiki/Spondylitis" title="Spondylitis">spondylitis</a> as the cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Most authorities today accept that Nightingale suffered from a particularly extreme form of brucellosis, the effects of which only began to lift in the early 1880s. Despite her symptoms, she remained phenomenally productive in social reform. During her bedridden years, she also did pioneering work in the field of hospital planning, and her work propagated quickly across Britain and the world. Nightingale's output slowed down considerably in her last decade. She wrote very little during that period due to blindness and declining mental abilities, though she still retained an interest in current affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NightingaleCrimeanColectedWorkds_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-NightingaleCrimeanColectedWorkds-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationships">Relationships</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Florence_Nightingale_by_Charles_Staal,_engraved_by_G._H._Mote.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="891" data-file-width="729" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Florence_Nightingale_by_Charles_Staal%2C_engraved_by_G._H._Mote.jpg/170px-Florence_Nightingale_by_Charles_Staal%2C_engraved_by_G._H._Mote.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Florence_Nightingale_by_Charles_Staal%2C_engraved_by_G._H._Mote.jpg/255px-Florence_Nightingale_by_Charles_Staal%2C_engraved_by_G._H._Mote.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Florence_Nightingale_by_Charles_Staal%2C_engraved_by_G._H._Mote.jpg/340px-Florence_Nightingale_by_Charles_Staal%2C_engraved_by_G._H._Mote.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Florence_Nightingale_by_Charles_Staal,_engraved_by_G._H._Mote.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Florence Nightingale by Charles Staal, engraved by G. H. Mote, used in <a href="/wiki/Mary_Cowden_Clarke" title="Mary Cowden Clarke">Mary Cowden Clarke</a>'s <i>Florence Nightingale</i> (1857)</div></div></div>
<p>Although much of Nightingale's work improved the lot of women everywhere, Nightingale believed that women craved <a href="/wiki/Sympathy" title="Sympathy">sympathy</a> and were not as capable as men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[a]</a></sup> She criticised early women's rights activists for decrying an alleged lack of careers for women at the same time that lucrative medical positions, under the supervision of Nightingale and others, went perpetually unfilled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[b]</a></sup> She preferred the friendship of powerful men, insisting they had done more than women to help her attain her goals, writing: "I have never found one woman who has altered her life by one iota for me or my opinions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NightingaleonWomen_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-NightingaleonWomen-62">[60]</a></sup> She often referred to herself in the masculine, as for example "a man of action" and "a man of business".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, she did have several important and long-lasting friendships with women. Later in life, she kept up a prolonged correspondence with Irish nun Sister Mary Clare Moore, with whom she had worked in Crimea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup> Her most beloved confidante was Mary Clarke, an Englishwoman she met in Paris in 1837 and kept in touch with throughout her life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some scholars of Nightingale's life believe that she remained chaste for her entire life, perhaps because she felt a religious calling to her career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:St_Margarets_FN_grave.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="760" data-file-width="570" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/St_Margarets_FN_grave.jpg/170px-St_Margarets_FN_grave.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/St_Margarets_FN_grave.jpg/255px-St_Margarets_FN_grave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/St_Margarets_FN_grave.jpg/340px-St_Margarets_FN_grave.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:St_Margarets_FN_grave.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The grave of Florence Nightingale in the churchyard of St Margaret's Church, <a href="/wiki/Wellow,_Hampshire" title="Wellow, Hampshire">East Wellow</a>, Hampshire</div></div></div>
<p>Florence Nightingale died peacefully in her sleep in her room at 10 <a href="/wiki/South_Street,_Mayfair" title="South Street, Mayfair">South Street, Mayfair</a>, London, on 13 August 1910, at the age of 90.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[c]</a></sup> The offer of burial in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> was declined by her relatives and she is buried in the churchyard of St Margaret's Church in <a href="/wiki/Wellow,_Hampshire" title="Wellow, Hampshire">East Wellow</a>, Hampshire, near Embley Park with a memorial with just her initials and dates of birth and death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup> She left a large body of work, including several hundred notes that were previously unpublished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelly_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelly-72">[69]</a></sup> A memorial monument to Nightingale was created in <a href="/wiki/Carrara_marble" title="Carrara marble">Carrara marble</a> by Francis William Sargant in 1913 and placed in the cloister of the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Croce,_Florence" title="Santa Croce, Florence">Basilica of Santa Croce</a>, in Florence, Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contributions">Contributions</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistics_and_sanitary_reform">Statistics and sanitary reform</span></h3>
<p>Florence Nightingale exhibited a gift for mathematics from an early age and excelled in the subject under the tutelage of her father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[d]</a></sup> Later, Nightingale became a pioneer in the visual presentation of information and <a href="/wiki/Statistical_graphics" title="Statistical graphics">statistical graphics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[72]</a></sup> She used methods such as the <a href="/wiki/Pie_chart" title="Pie chart">pie chart</a>, which had first been developed by <a href="/wiki/William_Playfair" title="William Playfair">William Playfair</a> in 1801.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[73]</a></sup> While taken for granted now, it was at the time a relatively novel method of presenting data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen1984_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen1984-78">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Indeed, Nightingale is described as "a true pioneer in the graphical representation of statistics", and is especially well-known for her usage of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_area_diagram" title="Polar area diagram">polar area diagram</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen1984_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen1984-78">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 107">: 107 </span></sup> or occasionally the <i>Nightingale rose diagram</i>, equivalent to a modern circular <a href="/wiki/Histogram" title="Histogram">histogram</a>, to illustrate seasonal sources of patient mortality in the military field hospital she managed.  While frequently credited as the creator of the polar area diagram, it is known to have been used by André-Michel Guerry in 1829<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[75]</a></sup> and Léon Louis Lalanne by 1830.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[76]</a></sup> Nightingale called a compilation of such diagrams a "coxcomb", but later that term would frequently be used for the individual diagrams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[77]</a></sup> She made extensive use of coxcombs to present reports on the nature and magnitude of the conditions of medical care in the Crimean War to <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Members of Parliament</a> and civil servants who would have been unlikely to read or understand traditional statistical reports. In 1859, Nightingale was elected the first female member of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Statistical_Society" title="Royal Statistical Society">Royal Statistical Society</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[78]</a></sup> In 1874 she became an honorary member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Statistical_Association" title="American Statistical Association">American Statistical Association</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nightingale-mortality.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3826" data-file-width="6996" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Nightingale-mortality.jpg/220px-Nightingale-mortality.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Nightingale-mortality.jpg/330px-Nightingale-mortality.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Nightingale-mortality.jpg/440px-Nightingale-mortality.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nightingale-mortality.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_area_diagram" title="Polar area diagram">Diagram</a> of the causes of mortality in the army in the East</i>" by Florence Nightingale</div></div></div>
<p>Her attention turned to the health of the British Army in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and she demonstrated that bad drainage, contaminated water, overcrowding, and poor ventilation were causing the high death rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[80]</a></sup> Following the report <i>The Royal Commission on India</i> (1858–1863), which included drawings done by her cousin, artist <a href="/wiki/Bonham_Carter_family" title="Bonham Carter family">Hilary Bonham Carter</a>, with whom Nightingale had lived,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[e]</a></sup> Nightingale concluded that the health of the army and the people of India had to go hand in hand and so campaigned to improve the sanitary conditions of the country as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_2017_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_2017-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nightingale made a comprehensive statistical study of <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a> in Indian rural life and was the leading figure in the introduction of improved medical care and public health service in India. In 1858 and 1859, she successfully lobbied for the establishment of a Royal Commission into the Indian situation. Two years later, she provided a report to the commission, which completed its own study in 1863. "After 10 years of sanitary reform, in 1873, Nightingale reported that mortality among the soldiers in India had declined from 69 to 18 per 1,000".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen1984_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen1984-78">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 107">: 107 </span></sup>
</p><p>The Royal Sanitary Commission of 1868–1869 presented Nightingale with an opportunity to press for compulsory sanitation in private houses. She lobbied the minister responsible, <a href="/wiki/James_Stansfeld" title="James Stansfeld">James Stansfeld</a>, to strengthen the proposed Public Health Bill to require owners of existing properties to pay for connection to mains drainage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[82]</a></sup> The strengthened legislation was enacted in the Public Health Acts of 1874 and 1875. At the same time, she combined with the retired sanitary reformer <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Chadwick" title="Edwin Chadwick">Edwin Chadwick</a> to persuade Stansfeld to devolve powers to enforce the law to Local Authorities, eliminating central control by medical technocrats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[83]</a></sup> Her Crimean War statistics had convinced her that non-medical approaches were more effective given the state of knowledge at the time. Historians now believe that both drainage and devolved enforcement played a crucial role in increasing average national life expectancy by 20 years between 1871 and the mid-1930s during which time medical science made no impact on the most fatal epidemic diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Constable1998_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Constable1998-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Florence_Nightingale_to_Her_Nurses_(1914)_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Florence_Nightingale_to_Her_Nurses_(1914)-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Literature_and_the_women.27s_movement"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature_and_the_women's_movement">Literature and the women's movement</span></h3>
<p>Historian of science <a href="/wiki/I._Bernard_Cohen" title="I. Bernard Cohen">I. Bernard Cohen</a> argues:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Nightingale's achievements are all the more impressive when they are considered against the background of social restraints on women in Victorian England. Her father, William Edward Nightingale, was an extremely wealthy landowner, and the family moved in the highest circles of English society. In those days, women of Nightingale's class did not attend universities and did not pursue professional careers; their purpose in life was to marry and bear children. Nightingale was fortunate. Her father believed women should be educated, and he personally taught her Italian, Latin, Greek, philosophy, history, and – most unusual of all for women of the time – writing and mathematics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen1984_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen1984-78">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 98">: 98 </span></sup></p></blockquote> <p><a href="/wiki/Lytton_Strachey" title="Lytton Strachey">Lytton Strachey</a> was famous for his book debunking 19th-century heroes, <i><a href="/wiki/Eminent_Victorians" title="Eminent Victorians">Eminent Victorians</a></i> (1918). Nightingale gets a full chapter, but instead of debunking her, Strachey praised her in a way that raised her national reputation and made her an icon for English feminists of the 1920s and 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>While better known for her contributions in the nursing and mathematical fields, Nightingale is also an important link in the study of English <a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">feminism</a>. She wrote some 200 books, pamphlets and articles throughout her life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Express_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Express-48">[48]</a></sup> During 1850 and 1852, she was struggling with her self-definition and the expectations of an upper-class marriage from her family. As she sorted out her thoughts, she wrote <i>Suggestions for Thought to Searchers after Religious Truth</i>. This was an 829-page, three-volume work, which Nightingale had printed privately in 1860, but which until recently was never published in its entirety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calabria&amp;Macrae1994_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Calabria&amp;Macrae1994-91">[86]</a></sup> An effort to correct this was made with a 2008 publication by <a href="/wiki/Wilfrid_Laurier_University" title="Wilfrid Laurier University">Wilfrid Laurier University</a>, as volume 11<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald2008_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald2008-92">[87]</a></sup> of a 16 volume project, the <i>Collected Works of Florence Nightingale</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WLUPress_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-WLUPress-93">[88]</a></sup> The best known of these essays, called "Cassandra", was previously published by <a href="/wiki/Ray_Strachey" title="Ray Strachey">Ray Strachey</a> in 1928. Strachey included it in <i>The Cause</i>, a history of the women's movement. Apparently, the writing served its original purpose of sorting out thoughts; Nightingale left soon after to train at the Institute for deaconesses at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaiserswerth" title="Kaiserswerth">Kaiserswerth</a>.
</p><p>"Cassandra" protests the over-feminisation of women into near helplessness, such as Nightingale saw in her mother's and older sister's lethargic lifestyle, despite their education. She rejected their life of thoughtless comfort for the world of social service. The work also reflects her fear of her ideas being ineffective, as were <a href="/wiki/Cassandra" title="Cassandra">Cassandra</a>'s. Cassandra was a princess of <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a> who served as a priestess in the temple of <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Trojan_War" title="Trojan War">Trojan War</a>. The god gave her the gift of <a href="/wiki/Prophecy" title="Prophecy">prophecy</a>; when she refused his advances, he cursed her so that her prophetic warnings would go unheeded. <a href="/wiki/Elaine_Showalter" title="Elaine Showalter">Elaine Showalter</a> called Nightingale's writing "a major text of English feminism, a link between <a href="/wiki/Mary_Wollstonecraft" title="Mary Wollstonecraft">Wollstonecraft</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf">Woolf</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[89]</a></sup> Nightingale was initially reluctant  to join the Women's <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Women's suffrage in the United Kingdom">Suffrage Society</a> when asked by <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>, but through <a href="/wiki/Josephine_Butler" title="Josephine Butler">Josephine Butler</a> was convinced 'that women's enfranchisement is absolutely essential to a nation if moral and social progress is to be made'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1972, the poet <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Ross_Taylor" title="Eleanor Ross Taylor">Eleanor Ross Taylor</a> wrote "Welcome Eumenides", a poem written in Nightingale's voice and quoting frequently from Nightingale's writings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Adrienne_Rich" title="Adrienne Rich">Adrienne Rich</a> wrote that "... Eleanor Taylor has brought together the waste of women in society and the waste of men in wars and twisted them inseparably."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology">Theology</span></h3>
<p>Despite being named as a Unitarian in several older sources, Nightingale's own rare references to conventional Unitarianism are mildly negative. She remained in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> throughout her life, albeit with unorthodox views. Influenced from an early age by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wesleyanism" title="Wesleyanism">Wesleyan tradition</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[f]</a></sup> Nightingale felt that genuine religion should manifest in active care and love for others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[g]</a></sup> She wrote a work of theology: <i>Suggestions for Thought</i>, her own <a href="/wiki/Theodicy" title="Theodicy">theodicy</a>, which develops her heterodox ideas. Nightingale questioned the goodness of a God who would condemn souls to hell and was a believer in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_reconciliation" title="Universal reconciliation">universal reconciliation</a> – the concept that even those who die without being saved will eventually make it to Heaven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[h]</a></sup> She would sometimes comfort those in her care with this view. For example, a dying young prostitute being tended by Nightingale was concerned she was going to hell and said to her "Pray God, that you may never be in the despair I am in at this time". The nurse replied "Oh, my girl, are you not now more merciful than the God you think you are going to? Yet the real God is far more merciful than any human creature ever was or can ever imagine."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NightingaleonMysticism_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-NightingaleonMysticism-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NightingaleonWomen_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-NightingaleonWomen-62">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[i]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[j]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite her intense personal devotion to Christ, Nightingale believed for much of her life that the pagan and eastern religions had also contained genuine revelation. She was a strong opponent of discrimination both against Christians of different denominations and against those of non-Christian religions.
Nightingale believed religion helped provide people with the fortitude for arduous good work and would ensure the nurses in her care attended religious services. However, she was often critical of organised religion. She disliked the role the 19th century Church of England would sometimes play in worsening the oppression of the poor. Nightingale argued that secular hospitals usually provided better care than their religious counterparts. While she held that the ideal health professional should be inspired by a religious as well as professional motive, she said that in practice many religiously motivated health workers were concerned chiefly in securing their own salvation and that this motivation was inferior to the professional desire to deliver the best possible care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NightingaleonMysticism_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-NightingaleonMysticism-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NightingaleonWomen_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-NightingaleonWomen-62">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nursing">Nursing</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nightingaleplaque.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Nightingaleplaque.JPG/220px-Nightingaleplaque.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Nightingaleplaque.JPG/330px-Nightingaleplaque.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Nightingaleplaque.JPG/440px-Nightingaleplaque.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nightingaleplaque.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Blue_plaque" title="Blue plaque">Blue plaque</a> for Nightingale in <a href="/wiki/South_Street,_Mayfair" title="South Street, Mayfair">South Street</a>, Mayfair, London</div></div></div>
<p>Nightingale's lasting contribution has been her role in founding the modern nursing profession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[96]</a></sup> She set an example of compassion, commitment to patient care and diligent and thoughtful hospital administration. The first official nurses' training programme, her <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nightingale_School_for_Nurses" title="Nightingale School for Nurses">Nightingale School for Nurses</a>, opened in 1860 and is now called the <a href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale_Faculty_of_Nursing_and_Midwifery" title="Florence Nightingale Faculty of Nursing and Midwifery">Florence Nightingale Faculty of Nursing and Midwifery</a> at <a href="/wiki/King%27s_College_London" title="King's College London">King's College London</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:30%; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>She belongs to that select band of historical characters who are instantly recognisable: the Lady with the Lamp, ministering to the wounded and dying.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">– <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> profile of Nightingale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_2017_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_2017-7">[7]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
