<p>In 1912, the <a href="/wiki/International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross" title="International Committee of the Red Cross">International Committee of the Red Cross</a> instituted the <a href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale_Medal" title="Florence Nightingale Medal">Florence Nightingale Medal</a>, which is awarded every two years to nurses or nursing aides for outstanding service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[98]</a></sup> It is the highest international distinction a nurse can achieve and is awarded to nurses or nursing aides for "exceptional courage and devotion to the wounded, sick or disabled or to civilian victims of a conflict or disaster" or "exemplary services or a creative and pioneering spirit in the areas of public health or nursing education".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[99]</a></sup> Since 1965, <a href="/wiki/International_Nurses_Day" title="International Nurses Day">International Nurses Day</a> has been celebrated on her birthday (12 May) each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[100]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/President_of_India" title="President of India">President of India</a> honours nursing professionals with the "National Florence Nightingale Award" every year on International Nurses Day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-India_Award_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-India_Award-111">[101]</a></sup> The award, established in 1973, is given in recognition of meritorious services of nursing professionals characterised by devotion, sincerity, dedication and compassion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-India_Award_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-India_Award-111">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pledge_of_Florence_Nightingale._Wellcome_L0008728.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1624" data-file-width="1158" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Pledge_of_Florence_Nightingale._Wellcome_L0008728.jpg/170px-Pledge_of_Florence_Nightingale._Wellcome_L0008728.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Pledge_of_Florence_Nightingale._Wellcome_L0008728.jpg/255px-Pledge_of_Florence_Nightingale._Wellcome_L0008728.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Pledge_of_Florence_Nightingale._Wellcome_L0008728.jpg/340px-Pledge_of_Florence_Nightingale._Wellcome_L0008728.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pledge_of_Florence_Nightingale._Wellcome_L0008728.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Nightingale_Pledge" title="Nightingale Pledge">Nightingale Pledge</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nightingale_Pledge" title="Nightingale Pledge">Nightingale Pledge</a> is a modified version of the <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Oath" title="Hippocratic Oath">Hippocratic Oath</a> which nurses recite at their <a href="/wiki/Pinning_ceremony_(nursing)" title="Pinning ceremony (nursing)">pinning ceremony</a> at the end of training. Created in 1893 and named after Nightingale as the founder of modern nursing, the pledge is a statement of the ethics and principles of the nursing profession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Florence Nightingale Declaration Campaign,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[103]</a></sup> established by nursing leaders throughout the world through the Nightingale Initiative for Global Health (NIGH), aims to build a global grassroots movement to achieve two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_Resolutions" title="United Nations Resolutions">United Nations Resolutions</a> for adoption by the UN General Assembly of 2008. They will declare: The International Year of the Nurse–2010 (the centenary of Nightingale's death); The UN Decade for a Healthy World – 2011 to 2020 (the bicentenary of Nightingale's birth). NIGH also works to rekindle awareness about the important issues highlighted by Florence Nightingale, such as preventive medicine and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holistic_health" title="Holistic health">holistic health</a>. As of 2016, the Florence Nightingale Declaration has been signed by over 25,000 signatories from 106 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>, Nightingale inspired many <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">US Army</a> nurses, sparking a renewal of interest in her life and work. Her admirers include <a href="/wiki/Country_Joe_McDonald" title="Country Joe McDonald">Country Joe</a> of <a href="/wiki/Country_Joe_and_the_Fish" title="Country Joe and the Fish">Country Joe and the Fish</a>, who has assembled an extensive website in her honour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[105]</a></sup> The Agostino Gemelli Medical School<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[106]</a></sup> in Rome, the first university-based hospital in Italy and one of its most respected medical centres, honoured Nightingale's contribution to the nursing profession by giving the name "Bedside Florence" to a wireless computer system it developed to assist nursing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hospitals">Hospitals</span></h3>
<p>Four hospitals in Istanbul are named after Nightingale: Florence Nightingale Hospital in <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Ei%C5%9Fli" title="Şişli">Şişli</a> (the biggest private hospital in Turkey), Metropolitan Florence Nightingale Hospital in Gayrettepe, European Florence Nightingale Hospital in <a href="/wiki/Mecidiyek%C3%B6y" title="Mecidiyeköy">Mecidiyeköy</a>, and Kızıltoprak Florence Nightingale Hospital in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kadik%C3%B6y" title="Kadiköy">Kadiköy</a>, all belonging to the Turkish Cardiology Foundation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011, an appeal was made for the former Derbyshire Royal Infirmary hospital in Derby, England to be named after Nightingale. It was suggested the name could be either Nightingale Community Hospital or Florence Nightingale Community Hospital. The area where the hospital is situated is sometimes referred to as the "Nightingale Quarter".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="COVID-19 pandemic in the United Kingdom">COVID-19 pandemic</a>, a number of temporary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NHS_Nightingale_Hospitals" title="NHS Nightingale Hospitals">NHS Nightingale Hospitals</a> were set up in readiness for an expected rise in the number of patients needing critical care. The first was housed in the <a href="/wiki/ExCeL_London" title="ExCeL London">ExCeL London</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[110]</a></sup> and several others followed across England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[111]</a></sup> Celebrations to mark her bicentenary in 2020, were disrupted by the coronavirus pandemic and Nightingale's contribution to scientific and statistical analysis of infectious disease and nursing practice may have led to the new temporary hospitals being in her name, in Scotland named the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NHS_Louisa_Jordan_Hospital" title="NHS Louisa Jordan Hospital">NHS Louisa Jordan</a> after a nurse who followed in Nightingale's footsteps in battlefield nursing in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_War_One" title="World War One">World War One</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-122">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Museums_and_monuments">Museums and monuments</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Florence_Nightingale_monument_London.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1553" data-file-width="855" decoding="async" height="309" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Florence_Nightingale_monument_London.jpg/170px-Florence_Nightingale_monument_London.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Florence_Nightingale_monument_London.jpg/255px-Florence_Nightingale_monument_London.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Florence_Nightingale_monument_London.jpg/340px-Florence_Nightingale_monument_London.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Florence_Nightingale_monument_London.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Nightingale by <a href="/wiki/Arthur_George_Walker" title="Arthur George Walker">Arthur George Walker</a> in Waterloo Place, London</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Florence_Nightingale_Statue,_London_Road,_Derby.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2896" data-file-width="1980" decoding="async" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Florence_Nightingale_Statue%2C_London_Road%2C_Derby.jpg/170px-Florence_Nightingale_Statue%2C_London_Road%2C_Derby.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Florence_Nightingale_Statue%2C_London_Road%2C_Derby.jpg/255px-Florence_Nightingale_Statue%2C_London_Road%2C_Derby.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Florence_Nightingale_Statue%2C_London_Road%2C_Derby.jpg/340px-Florence_Nightingale_Statue%2C_London_Road%2C_Derby.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Florence_Nightingale_Statue,_London_Road,_Derby.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Florence Nightingale Statue, <a href="/wiki/London_Road_(Sheffield)" title="London Road (Sheffield)">London Road</a>, <a href="/wiki/Derby" title="Derby">Derby</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Derby_DRI_stained_glass_window_at_St_Peters_squared.JPG"><img alt="A vertical rectangular stained glass window with nine panels, each holding one or more human figures" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3338" data-file-width="1760" decoding="async" height="322" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Derby_DRI_stained_glass_window_at_St_Peters_squared.JPG/170px-Derby_DRI_stained_glass_window_at_St_Peters_squared.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Derby_DRI_stained_glass_window_at_St_Peters_squared.JPG/255px-Derby_DRI_stained_glass_window_at_St_Peters_squared.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Derby_DRI_stained_glass_window_at_St_Peters_squared.JPG/340px-Derby_DRI_stained_glass_window_at_St_Peters_squared.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Derby_DRI_stained_glass_window_at_St_Peters_squared.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Florence Nightingale stained glass window, originally at the Derbyshire Royal Infirmary Chapel and now removed to <a href="/wiki/St_Peter%27s_Church,_Derby" title="St Peter's Church, Derby">St Peter's Church, Derby</a> and rededicated 9 October 2010</div></div></div>
<p>A statue of Florence Nightingale by the 20th-century war memorialist <a href="/wiki/Arthur_George_Walker" title="Arthur George Walker">Arthur George Walker</a> stands in Waterloo Place, <a href="/wiki/Westminster" title="Westminster">Westminster</a>, London, just off <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Mall_(London)" title="The Mall (London)">The Mall</a>. There are three statues of Nightingale in Derby – one outside the <a href="/wiki/Derbyshire_Royal_Infirmary" title="Derbyshire Royal Infirmary">Derbyshire Royal Infirmary</a> (DRI), one in St Peter's Street, and one above the Nightingale-Macmillan Continuing Care Unit opposite the Derbyshire Royal Infirmary. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_house" title="Public house">pub</a> named after her stands close to the DRI.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[113]</a></sup> The Nightingale-Macmillan continuing care unit is now at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Derby_Hospital" title="Royal Derby Hospital">Royal Derby Hospital</a>, formerly known as The City Hospital, Derby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stained_glass_window" title="Stained glass window">stained glass window</a> was commissioned for inclusion in the DRI chapel in the late 1950s. When the chapel was demolished the window was removed and installed in the replacement chapel. At the closure of the DRI, the window was again removed and stored. In October 2010, £6,000 was raised to reposition the window in <a href="/wiki/St_Peter%27s_Church,_Derby" title="St Peter's Church, Derby">St Peter's Church, Derby</a>. The work features nine panels, of the original ten, depicting scenes of hospital life, Derby townscapes, and Nightingale herself. Some of the work was damaged and the tenth panel was dismantled for the glass to be used in the repair of the remaining panels. All the figures, who are said to be modelled on prominent Derby town figures of the early sixties, surround and praise a central pane of the triumphant Christ. A nurse who posed for the top right panel in 1959 attended the rededication service in October 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale_Museum" title="Florence Nightingale Museum">Florence Nightingale Museum</a> at <a href="/wiki/St_Thomas%27_Hospital" title="St Thomas' Hospital">St Thomas' Hospital</a> in London reopened in May 2010 in time for the centenary of Nightingale's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Express_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-Express-48">[48]</a></sup> Another museum devoted to her is at her sister's family home, <a href="/wiki/Claydon_House" title="Claydon House">Claydon House</a>, now a property of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Trust_for_Places_of_Historic_Interest_or_Natural_Beauty" title="National Trust for Places of Historic Interest or Natural Beauty">National Trust</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Upon the centenary of Nightingale's death in 2010, and to commemorate her connection with <a href="/wiki/Malvern,_Worcestershire" title="Malvern, Worcestershire">Malvern</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Malvern_Museum" title="Malvern Museum">Malvern Museum</a> held a Florence Nightingale exhibit<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MalvernMuseum_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-MalvernMuseum-128">[118]</a></sup> with a school poster competition to promote some events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MalvernGazette21June2010_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-MalvernGazette21June2010-129">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Istanbul, the northernmost tower of the Selimiye Barracks building is now the Florence Nightingale Museum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NightingaleMuseumIstanbul_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-NightingaleMuseumIstanbul-130">[120]</a></sup> and in several of its rooms, relics and reproductions related to Florence Nightingale and her nurses are on exhibition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>When Nightingale moved on to the Crimea itself in May 1855, she often travelled on horseback to make hospital inspections. She later transferred to a mule cart and was reported to have escaped serious injury when the cart was toppled in an accident. Following this, she used a solid Russian-built black carriage, with a waterproof hood and curtains. The carriage was returned to England by <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Soyer" title="Alexis Soyer">Alexis Soyer</a> after the war and subsequently given to the Nightingale training school. The carriage was damaged when the hospital was bombed during the Second World War. It was restored and transferred to Claydon House and is now displayed at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Army_Medical_Services_Museum" title="Army Medical Services Museum">Army Medical Services Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mytchett" title="Mytchett">Mytchett</a>, Surrey, near <a href="/wiki/Aldershot" title="Aldershot">Aldershot</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Florence_Nightingale_bust_Gun_Hill_Park_2021.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3793" data-file-width="2844" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Florence_Nightingale_bust_Gun_Hill_Park_2021.jpg/170px-Florence_Nightingale_bust_Gun_Hill_Park_2021.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Florence_Nightingale_bust_Gun_Hill_Park_2021.jpg/255px-Florence_Nightingale_bust_Gun_Hill_Park_2021.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Florence_Nightingale_bust_Gun_Hill_Park_2021.jpg/340px-Florence_Nightingale_bust_Gun_Hill_Park_2021.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Florence_Nightingale_bust_Gun_Hill_Park_2021.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of Nightingale unveiled at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gun_Hill_Park" title="Gun Hill Park">Gun Hill Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Aldershot" title="Aldershot">Aldershot</a> in 2021</div></div></div>
<p>A bronze plaque, attached to the plinth of the Crimean Memorial in the <a href="/wiki/Haydarpa%C5%9Fa_Cemetery" title="Haydarpaşa Cemetery">Haydarpaşa Cemetery</a>, Istanbul, Turkey and unveiled on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empire_Day" title="Empire Day">Empire Day</a>, 1954, to celebrate the 100th anniversary of her nursing service in that region, bears the inscription: "To Florence Nightingale, whose work near this Cemetery a century ago relieved much human suffering and laid the foundations for the nursing profession."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[123]</a></sup> Other monuments of Nightingale include a statue at <a href="/wiki/Chiba_University" title="Chiba University">Chiba University</a> in Japan, a bust at <a href="/wiki/Tarlac_State_University" title="Tarlac State University">Tarlac State University</a> in the Philippines, and a bust at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gun_Hill_Park" title="Gun Hill Park">Gun Hill Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Aldershot" title="Aldershot">Aldershot</a> in the UK. Other nursing schools around the world are named after Nightingale, such as in <a href="/wiki/An%C3%A1polis" title="Anápolis">Anápolis</a> in Brazil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Audio">Audio</span></h3>
<p>Florence Nightingale's voice was saved for posterity in a <a href="/wiki/Phonograph" title="Phonograph">phonograph</a> recording from 1890 preserved in the <a href="/wiki/British_Library_Sound_Archive" title="British Library Sound Archive">British Library Sound Archive</a>. The recording, made in aid of the <a href="/wiki/Charge_of_the_Light_Brigade" title="Charge of the Light Brigade">Light Brigade Relief Fund</a> and available to hear online, says:
</p>
<blockquote><p>When I am no longer even a memory, just a name, I hope my voice may perpetuate the great work of my life. God bless my dear old comrades of Balaclava and bring them safe to shore. Florence Nightingale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[125]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theatre">Theatre</span></h3>
<p>The first theatrical representation of Nightingale was <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Berkeley" title="Reginald Berkeley">Reginald Berkeley</a>'s <i>The Lady with the Lamp</i>, premiering in London in 1929 with <a href="/wiki/Edith_Evans" title="Edith Evans">Edith Evans</a> in the title role. It did not portray her as an entirely sympathetic character and draws much characterisation from <a href="/wiki/Lytton_Strachey" title="Lytton Strachey">Lytton Strachey</a>'s biography of her in <i><a href="/wiki/Eminent_Victorians" title="Eminent Victorians">Eminent Victorians</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[126]</a></sup> It was adapted as a film of the same name in 1951. In 2009, a stage musical play representation of Nightingale entitled <i>The Voyage of the Lass</i> was produced by the Association of Nursing Service Administrators of the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film">Film</span></h3>
<p>In 1912, a biographical silent film titled <i>The Victoria Cross</i>, starring <a href="/wiki/Julia_Swayne_Gordon" title="Julia Swayne Gordon">Julia Swayne Gordon</a> as Nightingale, was released, followed in 1915 by another silent film, <i><a href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale_(1915_film)" title="Florence Nightingale (1915 film)">Florence Nightingale</a></i>, featuring <a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_Risdon" title="Elisabeth Risdon">Elisabeth Risdon</a>. In 1936, <a href="/wiki/Kay_Francis" title="Kay Francis">Kay Francis</a> played Nightingale in the film titled <i><a href="/wiki/The_White_Angel_(1936_film)" title="The White Angel (1936 film)">The White Angel</a></i>. In 1951, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lady_with_a_Lamp" title="The Lady with a Lamp">The Lady with a Lamp</a></i> starred <a href="/wiki/Anna_Neagle" title="Anna Neagle">Anna Neagle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[127]</a></sup>
In 1993, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nest_Entertainment" title="Nest Entertainment">Nest Entertainment</a> released an animated film <i>Florence Nightingale</i>, describing her service as a nurse in the Crimean War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Television">Television</span></h3>
<p>Portrayals of Nightingale on television, in documentary as in fiction, vary – the BBC's 2008 <i><a href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale_(2008_film)" title="Florence Nightingale (2008 film)">Florence Nightingale</a></i>, featuring <a href="/wiki/Laura_Fraser" title="Laura Fraser">Laura Fraser</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[129]</a></sup> emphasised her independence and feeling of religious calling, but in Channel 4's 2006 <i><a href="/wiki/Mary_Seacole" title="Mary Seacole">Mary Seacole</a>: The Real Angel of the Crimea</i>, she is portrayed as narrow-minded and opposed to Seacole's efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other portrayals include:
</p>
<ul><li>Laura Morgan in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victoria_(UK_TV_series)" title="Victoria (UK TV series)">Victoria</a></i> episode #3.4 "Foreign Bodies" (2018)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[131]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kate_Isitt" title="Kate Isitt">Kate Isitt</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/Magic_Grandad" title="Magic Grandad">Magic Grandad</a></i> episode "Famous People: Florence Nightingale" (1994)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[132]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jaclyn_Smith" title="Jaclyn Smith">Jaclyn Smith</a> in the TV biopic <i>Florence Nightingale</i> (1985)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[133]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Emma_Thompson" title="Emma Thompson">Emma Thompson</a> in the ITV sketch comedy series <i><a href="/wiki/Alfresco_(TV_series)" title="Alfresco (TV series)">Alfresco</a></i> episode #1.2 (1983)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[134]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jayne_Meadows" title="Jayne Meadows">Jayne Meadows</a> in PBS series <i><a href="/wiki/Meeting_of_Minds" title="Meeting of Minds">Meeting of Minds</a></i> (1978)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[135]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Janet_Suzman" title="Janet Suzman">Janet Suzman</a> in the British theatre-style biopic <i>Miss Nightingale</i> (1974)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[136]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julie_Harris_(actress)" title="Julie Harris (actress)">Julie Harris</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Hallmark_Hall_of_Fame" title="Hallmark Hall of Fame">Hallmark Hall of Fame</a></i> episode #14.4  "<a href="/wiki/List_of_Hallmark_Hall_of_Fame_episodes" title="List of Hallmark Hall of Fame episodes">The Holy Terror</a>" (1965)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[137]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sarah_Churchill_(actress)" title="Sarah Churchill (actress)">Sarah Churchill</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Hallmark_Hall_of_Fame" title="Hallmark Hall of Fame">Hallmark Hall of Fame</a></i> episode #1.6 "<a href="/wiki/List_of_Hallmark_Hall_of_Fame_episodes" title="List of Hallmark Hall of Fame episodes">Florence Nightingale</a>" (1952)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[138]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Banknotes">Banknotes</span></h3>
<p>Florence Nightingale's image appeared on the reverse of <a href="/wiki/Banknotes_of_the_pound_sterling#Historical_figures" title="Banknotes of the pound sterling">£10 Series D banknotes</a> issued by the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England_note_issues" title="Bank of England note issues">Bank of England</a> from 1975 until 1994. As well as a standing portrait, she was depicted on the notes in a field hospital, holding her lamp.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bankofengland_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-bankofengland-149">[139]</a></sup> Nightingale's note was in circulation alongside the images of <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">Michael Faraday</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Christopher_Wren" title="Sir Christopher Wren">Sir Christopher Wren</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">Duke of Wellington</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Stephenson" title="George Stephenson">George Stephenson</a>, and prior to 2002, other than the female monarchs, she was the only woman whose image had ever adorned British paper currency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_2017_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_2017-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photographs">Photographs</span></h3>
<p>Nightingale had a principled objection to having photographs taken or her portrait painted. An extremely rare photograph of her, taken at Embley on a visit to her family home in May 1858, was discovered in 2006 and is now at the <a href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale_Museum" title="Florence Nightingale Museum">Florence Nightingale Museum</a> in London. A black-and-white photograph taken in about 1907 by <a href="/wiki/Lizzie_Caswall_Smith" title="Lizzie Caswall Smith">Lizzie Caswall Smith</a> at Nightingale's London home in South Street, Mayfair, was auctioned on 19 November 2008 by Dreweatts auction house in Newbury, Berkshire, England, for £5,500.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-photograph_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-photograph-150">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biographies">Biographies</span></h3>
<p>The first biography of Nightingale was published in England in 1855. In 1911, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Tyas_Cook" title="Edward Tyas Cook">Edward Tyas Cook</a> was authorised by Nightingale's executors to write the official life, published in two volumes in 1913. Nightingale was also the subject of one of <a href="/wiki/Lytton_Strachey" title="Lytton Strachey">Lytton Strachey</a>'s four mercilessly provocative biographical essays, <i><a href="/wiki/Eminent_Victorians" title="Eminent Victorians">Eminent Victorians</a></i>. Strachey regarded Nightingale as an intense, driven woman who was both personally intolerable and admirable in her achievements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cecil_Woodham-Smith" title="Cecil Woodham-Smith">Cecil Woodham-Smith</a>, like Strachey, relied heavily on Cook's <i>Life</i> in her 1950 biography, though she did have access to new family material preserved at Claydon. In 2008, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Bostridge" title="Mark Bostridge">Mark Bostridge</a> published a major new life of Nightingale, almost exclusively based on unpublished material from the Verney Collections at Claydon and from archival documents from about 200 archives around the world, some of which had been published by Lynn McDonald in her projected sixteen-volume edition of the <i>Collected Works of Florence Nightingale</i> (2001 to date).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_2017_7-7"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_2017-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KLM_MD_11_AMS.jpg"><img alt="A three-engine wide-body jet airliner in blue and gray livery" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1799" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/KLM_MD_11_AMS.jpg/220px-KLM_MD_11_AMS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/KLM_MD_11_AMS.jpg/330px-KLM_MD_11_AMS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/KLM_MD_11_AMS.jpg/440px-KLM_MD_11_AMS.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KLM_MD_11_AMS.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/KLM" title="KLM">KLM</a> <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_MD-11" title="McDonnell Douglas MD-11">MD-11</a>, registration PH-KCD, <i>Florence Nightingale</i></div></div></div>
<p>In 2002, Nightingale was ranked number 52 in the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>'s list of the <a href="/wiki/100_Greatest_Britons" title="100 Greatest Britons">100 Greatest Britons</a> following a UK-wide vote. In 2006, the Japanese public ranked Nightingale number 17 in <a href="/wiki/The_Top_100_Historical_Persons_in_Japan" title="The Top 100 Historical Persons in Japan">The Top 100 Historical Persons in Japan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several churches in the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a> commemorate Nightingale with a feast day on their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liturgical_calendar" title="Liturgical calendar">liturgical calendars</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Lutheran_Church_in_America" title="Evangelical Lutheran Church in America">Evangelical Lutheran Church in America</a> commemorates her as a <a href="/wiki/Renewer_of_Society" title="Renewer of Society">Renewer of Society</a> with <a href="/wiki/Clara_Maass" title="Clara Maass">Clara Maass</a> on 13 August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Washington_National_Cathedral" title="Washington National Cathedral">Washington National Cathedral</a> celebrates Nightingale's accomplishments with a double-lancet stained glass window featuring six scenes from her life, designed by artist Joseph G. Reynolds and installed in 1983.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">US Navy</a> ship the <a href="/wiki/USS_Florence_Nightingale_(AP-70)" title="USS Florence Nightingale (AP-70)">USS <i>Florence Nightingale</i> <span class="nowrap">(AP-70)</span></a> was commissioned in 1942. Beginning in 1968, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">US Air Force</a> operated a fleet of 20 <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_C-9" title="McDonnell Douglas C-9">C-9A "Nightingale"</a> <a href="/wiki/Medical_evacuation" title="Medical evacuation">aeromedical evacuation</a> aircraft, based on the <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_DC-9" title="McDonnell Douglas DC-9">McDonnell Douglas DC-9</a> platform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[145]</a></sup> The last of these planes was retired from service in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1981, the asteroid <a href="/wiki/3122_Florence" title="3122 Florence">3122 Florence</a> was named after her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[147]</a></sup> A Dutch <a href="/wiki/KLM" title="KLM">KLM</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McDonnell-Douglas_MD-11" title="McDonnell-Douglas MD-11">McDonnell-Douglas MD-11</a> (registration PH-KCD) was also named in her honour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[148]</a></sup> Nightingale has appeared on international postage stamps, including, the UK, <a href="/wiki/List_of_postage_stamps_of_Alderney" title="List of postage stamps of Alderney">Alderney</a>, Australia, Belgium, Dominica, Hungary (showing the Florence Nightingale medal awarded by the International Red Cross), and Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>Florence Nightingale is <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Church_of_England)" title="Calendar of saints (Church of England)">remembered</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commemoration_(observance)" title="Commemoration (observance)">commemoration</a> on 13 August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[150]</a></sup> Celebrations to mark her bicentenary in 2020, were disrupted by the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">coronavirus pandemic</a>, but the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_hospitals_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="COVID-19 hospitals in the United Kingdom">NHS Nightingale</a> hospitals were named after her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-122">[112]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Footnotes">Footnotes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In an 1861 letter Nightingale wrote, "<i>Women have no sympathy.</i> ... Women crave for being loved, not for loving. They scream out at you for sympathy all day long, they are incapable of giving any in return, for they cannot remember your affairs long enough to do so. ... They cannot state a fact accurately to another, nor can that other attend to it accurately enough for it to become information."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-60">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the same 1861 letter she wrote, "It makes me mad, the Women's Rights talk about 'the want of a field' for them – when I would gladly give £500 a year for a Woman secretary. And two English Lady superintendents have told me the same thing. And we can't get <i>one</i> ..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Florence Nightingale, the famous nurse of the Crimean war and the only woman who ever received the Order of Merit, died yesterday afternoon at her London home. Although she had been an invalid for a long time, rarely leaving her room, where she passed the time in a half-recumbent position and was under the constant care of a physician, her death was somewhat unexpected. A week ago she was quite sick, but then improved and on Friday was cheerful. During that night alarming symptoms developed and she gradually sank until 2 o'clock Saturday afternoon, when the end came. — <i>New York Times</i> (15 Aug 1910)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There were rumors that she was tutored by an eminent mathematician who was a friend of the family. Mark Bostridge says, "There appears to be no documentary evidence to connect Florence with J. J. Sylvester."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-86"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-86">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">[many letters were written by Nightingale to her cousin Hilary Bonham-Carter] ... Royal Commission on India (1858–1863) ... feeling that her cousin was neglecting her art, [Nightingale] made Hilary Bonham Carter leave ... the Indian embroidery belonged to dear Hilary ...<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[81]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-98"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-98">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Her parents took their daughters to both Church of England and Methodist churches.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-100"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-100">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Nightingale's rare references to Unitarianism are mildly negative, and while her religious views were heterodox, she remained in the Church of England throughout her life. Her biblical annotations, private journal notes, and translations of the mystics give quite a different impression of her beliefs, and these do have a bearing on her work with nurses, and not only at Edinburgh, but neither [Cecil(ia) Woodham-]Smith nor [her] followers consulted their sources."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[93]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-101"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-101">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">While this has changed by the 21st century, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_reconciliation" title="Universal reconciliation">universal reconciliation</a></i> was very far from being mainstream in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> at the time.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-103"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-103">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Certainly the worst man would hardly torture his enemy, if he could, forever. Unless God has a scheme that every man is to be saved forever, it is hard to say in what He is not worse than man. For all good men would save others if they could."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[94]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-105"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-105">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although not formally a Universalist by church membership, she had come of a Universalist family, was sympathetic to the tenets of the denomination, and has always been claimed by it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[95]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
